reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, num_classes=1000):
        """
        Initialize the VGG19 model.

        :param num_classes: The number of output classes (default is 1000 for ImageNet)
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        
        # VGG19 architecture: 16 Conv layers + 5 MaxPool layers + 3 Fully Connected layers
        self.features = nn.Sequential(
            # Block 1
            nn.Conv2d(3, 64, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(64, 64, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2),
            
            # Block 2
            nn.Conv2d(64, 128, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(128, 128, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2),
            
            # Block 3
            nn.Conv2d(128, 256, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(256, 256, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(256, 256, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(256, 256, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2),
            
            # Block 4
            nn.Conv2d(256, 512, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(512, 512, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(512, 512, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(512, 512, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2),
            
            # Block 5
            nn.Conv2d(512, 512, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(512, 512, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(512, 512, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(512, 512, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2)
        )
        
        self.classifier = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Linear(512 * 7 * 7, 4096),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Dropout(p=0.0),
            nn.Linear(4096, 4096),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Dropout(p=0.0),
            nn.Linear(4096, num_classes)
        )
    
    def forward(self, x):
        """
        Forward pass of the VGG19 model.

        :param x: The input tensor, shape (batch_size, 3, 224, 224)
        :return: The output tensor, shape (batch_size, num_classes)
        """
        x = self.features(x)
        x = torch.flatten(x, 1)
        x = self.classifier(x)
        return x

# Test code
batch_size = 10
num_classes = 1000

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, 3, 224, 224)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [num_classes]

We need to replace the forward computation of a VGG19 model with a custom CUDA kernel. The model includes a lot of conv2d + relu, maxpool2d, and fully connected layers. Writing a monolithic CUDA kernel for the entire model is a huge undertaking. But we can potentially write a custom CUDA kernel for the entire VGG19 architecture: all convolutions, ReLUs, maxpooling, and the final linear layers fused into one kernel? That seems unrealistic for one kernel due to the huge number of parameters and intermediate feature maps. However, we can use a combination of custom kernels and PyTorch ops, but the requirement says "Replace the forward computation of the PyTorch model below with a custom CUDA kernel. Your ModelNew class must: ... Implement forward() with the same signature. Produce numerically equivalent outputs at fp32 precision. Run faster than the PyTorch reference." We are not forced to write a single kernel for everything; we can write multiple kernels and call them. But the spirit is to replace the forward with custom CUDA. Given the constraints, maybe we can use cudnn for convolutions, but the reference already uses PyTorch which uses cudnn. To be faster, we need to fuse operations: conv+relu, conv+relu+maxpool, and the classifier. We can write custom kernels that fuse some layers, but implementing a full convolution kernel from scratch that beats cuDNN is extremely hard. However, we might not need to beat cuDNN on each conv; we can fuse multiple layers to reduce memory traffic. For VGG19, the main bottleneck is memory bandwidth for the intermediate activations, especially since the conv layers are compute-bound but the ReLU and maxpool and the large feature maps are memory-bound. If we fuse a conv+relu into one kernel, we can reduce memory writes. Similarly, we can fuse conv+relu+maxpool. But implementing a fused conv+relu kernel using im2col and GEMM or direct convolution is complex. Alternatively, we can try to use PyTorch's `torch.nn.functional.conv2d` but with `torch._C._nn.thnn_conv2d`? No, that might be slower. Alternative approach: Use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` to compile a CUDA extension that calls cuDNN or uses CUTLASS? But we can't easily link cuDNN or CUTLASS via load_inline unless the headers are available. The environment likely has cuDNN, but writing a cuDNN wrapper is also complex. We need to be numerically equivalent (fp32) and faster. Given the H100 specs, we can use shared memory, cuDNN-style convolution, or perhaps just use cuBLAS/cuDNN? We can call cuDNN through PyTorch, but the point is to write a custom CUDA kernel replacing the forward computation. Since the reference uses PyTorch's nn.Conv2d and nn.Linear, it likely calls cudnn/cublas under the hood. Our custom kernel might not be able to beat PyTorch's highly optimized cuDNN for individual conv layers unless we fuse operations: conv + ReLU, fuse maxpool, flatten, and perhaps do the entire inference as a sequence of fused kernels or a single kernel per stage. But we might also be able to beat PyTorch by fusing more and reducing memory traffic. However, implementing a full VGG19 forward pass with all conv layers as a custom CUDA kernel from scratch is extremely complex. Kernel for fused conv + bias + ReLU + optional maxpool: For each output pixel (b, oc, oh, ow): - If no maxpool: compute conv directly. - If maxpool: we need to compute conv for a 2x2 neighborhood and take max after ReLU. But computing maxpool in the same kernel requires computing 4 output pixels (or more) and reducing. This is doable but complex. Alternatively, we can just fuse conv+relu and call a separate maxpool kernel. Fusing maxpool saves a write+read of the conv output. But maxpool on H100 is very fast; maybe not a big win. The biggest win is fusing conv+relu. cuDNN can already fuse conv+bias+relu (maybe with `cudnnConvolutionBiasActivationForward`), especially if we set `torch.backends.cudnn.benchmark = True`. PyTorch's `nn.Conv2d` + `nn.ReLU` may not be fused automatically. Actually, PyTorch's `nn.ReLU(inplace=True)` after `nn.Conv2d` might be fused by the JIT? No, in eager mode, they are separate kernels. cuDNN can fuse conv+bias+relu if PyTorch uses the fused op `cudnn_convolution_relu`. But in a `nn.Sequential` with separate modules, PyTorch calls `conv2d` then `relu` separately. However, `torch.backends.cudnn.benchmark=True` might help but still separate calls. cuDNN's fused API may be used if `torch.nn.Conv2d` uses the native `convolution` dispatch, which does not fuse with ReLU unless `torch.nn.intrinsic` (like `ConvReLU2d`) is used. The reference uses separate modules. So there are ~20 separate conv kernels and ~16 separate ReLU kernels and ~5 separate maxpool kernels. If we write a kernel that fuses conv+bias+relu, we can eliminate the ReLU kernel launches and memory traffic. That alone might give a decent speedup because the activation I/O for ReLU is significant: e.g., conv1 output is 320MB, reading and writing it for ReLU is 640MB of traffic. Fusing saves that. For all layers, the total saved traffic is roughly sum of all activation sizes (since ReLU reads then writes). That's ~1.5GB of traffic saved. At 3.35 TB/s, that's ~0.45 ms saved. If the total reference time is ~5 ms, that's a 9% speedup. Not huge, but maybe enough. We can also fuse maxpool: after conv2+relu, instead of writing 320MB and reading it for maxpool to write 80MB, we compute conv2+relu directly into registers and maxpool on the fly, writing only 80MB. That saves another 320MB write + 320MB read = 640MB traffic. So for each block, we save a lot. For block1, fusing conv1+relu+conv2+relu+maxpool into one kernel would save writing conv1 output (320MB), conv2 output (320MB), and reading them. The kernel only reads input (1.5MB) and writes pool output (80MB). That's huge. But implementing a kernel that computes two convs in sequence without intermediate global memory is possible but tricky. Let's analyze block1 in detail: Input: B x 3 x 224 x 224 Conv1: 3 -> 64. After conv1+relu: B x 64 x 224 x 224. Conv2: 64 -> 64. After conv2+relu: B x 64 x 224 x 224. MaxPool: B x 64 x 112 x 112. To fuse these, a thread block could process a tile of the final output, say a region of 112x112 spatial after maxpool. But the maxpool is 2x2. So the pre-pool spatial region is 224x224. We could tile over the 224x224 spatial domain. For a tile of 16x16 output pixels (before pool) or 8x8 after pool. Alternatively, we can compute the output of conv2+relu+maxpool for a tile of the final pooled output. For each pooled pixel (h_pool, w_pool), it corresponds to 2x2 pre-pool pixels. For each pre-pool pixel, we need conv2 output. To compute conv2 output at (h,w), we need conv1 output in a 3x3 neighborhood around (h,w). So to compute a 2x2 tile of pre-pool pixels, we need conv1 output in a 4x4 region (since 2x2 with 3x3 kernel needs input from (h-1..h+2, w-1..w+2)). And to compute conv1 output for a 4x4 region, we need input in a 6x6 region. So for a 2x2 pooled output tile, we need input 6x6. If we tile larger, say 8x8 pooled output = 16x16 pre-pool. Actually, for each (dy,dx) offset in 3x3, the contribution to output is `input[:, c, h+dy, w+dx] * weight[k, c, dy, dx]`. Summed over c and dy,dx. So for each (dy,dx), we can compute a matrix product: - A: (N*H*W) x C, where each row is input vector at a spatial position shifted by (dy,dx). But because of padding, the shifted positions have zeros at boundaries. - B: C x K, where B[c, k] = weight[k, c, dy, dx]. - Then output += A @ B. This is a GEMM of size (N*H*W) x C times C x K. For N=10, H=W=224, M=501760, C=64, K=64. This is a GEMM of 501k x 64 x 64. That's tiny for cublas. But we can sum the 9 contributions. We could call `unfold` in PyTorch, but that's just PyTorch. So back to the fused kernel idea. Maybe we can write a generic kernel that uses a for-loop over output channels and input channels. Let's think of a kernel with these parameters: - `TILE_H`, `TILE_W`: spatial tile processed per block. - `BLOCK_C`: input channel tile. - `BLOCK_K`: output channel tile. A single block loops over input channel blocks and computes a `BLOCK_K x TILE_H x TILE_W` output tile. Shared memory: - `s_in[BLOCK_C][TILE_H+2][TILE_W+2]`: input tile for current channel block. - `s_w[BLOCK_K][BLOCK_C][3][3]`: weight tile for current channel block. Each thread is responsible for computing a subset of the output tile (e.g., one spatial pixel and a few output channels). Let's calculate shared memory size: For `BLOCK_C = 16`, `TILE_H = 8`, `TILE_W = 8`: - s_in: 16 * 10 * 10 = 1600 floats = 6.4KB. - s_w: 16 * 16 * 9 = 2304 floats = 9.2KB. Total ~15.6KB. Very small. If `BLOCK_K = 64`, `BLOCK_C = 8`, `TILE_H = 16`, `TILE_W = 16`: - s_in: 8 * 18 * 18 = 2592 floats = 10.4KB. - s_w: 64 * 8 * 9 = 4608 floats = 18.4KB. Total ~29KB. Still small. If we want better occupancy, we can increase tile sizes. Let's try `BLOCK_K = 128`, `BLOCK_C = 16`, `TILE_H = 8`, `TILE_W = 8`: - s_in: 16 * 10 * 10 = 1600 = 6.4KB. - s_w: 128 * 16 * 9 = 18432 = 73.7KB. Total ~80KB. Good. Each block computes 128 output channels for an 8x8 spatial tile. The number of blocks per layer: `ceil(K/128) * ceil(H/8) * ceil(W/8) * N`. For layer 1: K=64 => 1 group. H=224/8=28, W=224/8=28. N=10. Total blocks = 1*28*28*10 = 7840. That's plenty. For layer 2: K=64. Same number of blocks. For layer 5: K=256 => 2 groups. H=56/8=7. N=10. Blocks = 2*7*7*10 = 980. For layer 9: K=512 => 4 groups. H=28/8=4 (ceil to 4). Blocks = 4*4*4*10 = 640. So occupancy is decent. Now, the kernel logic: For each output pixel (n, k, h, w) assigned to a thread: ```cpp float sum = bias[k]; for c_block = 0; c_block < C; c_block += BLOCK_C: // Load s_in for this c_block and spatial tile // Load s_w for this k_block and c_block __syncthreads(); for c = 0..BLOCK_C-1: for dy = 0..2: for dx = 0..2: sum += s_in[c][thread_h+dy][thread_w+dx] * s_w[k_local][c][dy][dx]; __syncthreads(); out = max(sum, 0.0f); // ReLU ``` But we need to assign threads to load data. We have `BLOCK_K * TILE_H * TILE_W` total output elements per block. If we have `blockDim.x = TILE_H * TILE_W` threads (64), and each thread computes `BLOCK_K / (blockDim.x / (TILE_H*TILE_W))`? Actually, we can have `blockDim.x = 128` or `256` threads. Let's say `blockDim.x = 128` threads. Then each thread computes `(BLOCK_K * TILE_H * TILE_W) / 128` output elements. But the weight loading needs to be cooperative: all threads load the `BLOCK_K * BLOCK_C * 9` weights into shared memory. The input loading needs `BLOCK_C * (TILE_H+2) * (TILE_W+2)` elements. We need to be careful with memory access patterns: input loading should be coalesced. Threads can load input channels in parallel. Alternatively, we can simplify by having each thread compute `BLOCK_K` output channels for one spatial pixel. Then `blockDim.x = TILE_H * TILE_W` threads. Then each thread loops over `BLOCK_C` and `3x3` to accumulate. This way, the input is shared across all threads (they all need the same input tile), so we load it into shared memory once. The weights are also loaded into shared memory. Since each thread computes all `BLOCK_K` channels, they all read the same input element but different weights. That's a lot of weight reads from shared memory, but shared memory bandwidth is high (bank conflicts might be an issue). Let's analyze: `TILE_H = 8`, `TILE_W = 8` => 64 threads. `BLOCK_K = 128`. `BLOCK_C = 16`. Each thread computes 128 output values (one spatial pixel, 128 channels). Per thread, inner loop: for c in 0..15, for dy,dx in 0..2: read input (float), read weight (float). That's 16*9 = 144 iterations. Each iteration does 1 FMA. So 144 FMAs per thread per block channel loop. Total FMAs per thread = (C/16) * 144. For C=512, that's 4608 FMAs. For 64 threads, 294,912 FMAs per block. Block run time depends on occupancy. Shared memory loads for weights: 128 threads? No, only 64 threads. But 64 threads can load 128*16*9 = 18432 floats. Each thread loads 18432/64 = 288 floats. That's okay. Potential bank conflicts: weights are accessed as `s_w[k_local][c][dy][dx]`. If we store as `s_w[BLOCK_K][BLOCK_C][9]`, then in the inner loop over c and dy,dx, threads access `k_local` differently (each thread has its own `k_local`). If each thread handles a range of k (e.g., thread 0 handles k=0..1, thread 1 handles k=2..3, etc.), then adjacent threads access adjacent k. The access pattern across threads is strided by `BLOCK_C*9`. If `BLOCK_C*9 = 144`, and each float is 4 bytes, then adjacent k are 576 bytes apart. Shared memory banks are 4 bytes wide on H100? Actually shared memory banks are 4 bytes wide. So 576 / 4 = 144 banks. With 32 banks, 144 mod 32 = 16. So threads in a warp access k values that are 144 apart, which might cause some conflicts but not terrible. We can transpose weight storage: `s_w[BLOCK_C][9][BLOCK_K]`. Then for a given c and offset, threads access different k but the same c and offset. The index is `s_w[c][dy*3+dx][k_local]`. Adjacent `k_local` are adjacent in memory, so no bank conflicts! Great. Similarly, input storage: `s_in[BLOCK_C][TILE_H+2][TILE_W+2]`. When threads load input for a given c and spatial position, they all read the same spatial position but different c? Actually, each thread reads input for its own spatial pixel, and for each c. If we store as `s_in[BLOCK_C][H][W]`, then for a given c, threads access different (h,w). That's fine. But for a fixed (h,w) and varying c, threads access different c. If we instead store as `s_in[H][W][BLOCK_C]`, then for a fixed (h,w) (which is same across threads? No, each thread has its own (h,w)), and varying c, the accesses are strided by 1. If threads in a warp have different (h,w) but same c, then they access `s_in[h_i][w_i][c]`. The stride between (h_i,w_i) might not be coalesced in shared memory. But shared memory doesn't need coalescing; it needs bank conflict avoidance. If `W` is not a multiple of bank width? `s_in[H][W][BLOCK_C]` means index = (h*W + w)*BLOCK_C + c. If BLOCK_C is a power of 2 (e.g., 16), then adjacent threads with same h,w but different c might conflict? Actually, each thread reads a sequence of c values in a loop. Threads in a warp might all read `c=0` first, but at different (h,w). The address for each thread is (h_i*W + w_i)*BLOCK_C + 0. If (h_i*W + w_i) are different, then the lower bits determine bank. Since BLOCK_C=16, address = base*16. So bank = (base*16 / 4) % 32 = base*4 % 32. So bank depends on base modulo 8. If multiple threads have base modulo 8 equal, they conflict. With 64 threads (2 warps), many may conflict. To avoid this, we can pad the W dimension. Or store as `s_in[BLOCK_C][H][W]` (i.e., channel-major). Then address = c*H*W + h*W + w. For a given c, threads access different h and w, and the address increment is 1 for adjacent w, which are in different banks. So no conflicts for same c. But threads loop over c. For consecutive c, the stride is H*W. If H*W=100, then bank changes by 100 mod 32 = 4. So consecutive c for the same thread hit different banks. No conflicts within a thread. Across threads, they are all reading the same c at the same time (since loop over c is uniform). So all threads in a warp read `s_in[c][h][w]` for the same c but different (h,w). With `s_in[BLOCK_C][H][W]`, the addresses are `c*H*W + h_i*W + w_i`. The lower bits are determined by `h_i*W + w_i`. If W is a multiple of 32? Let's see: W = TILE_W+2. For TILE_W=8, W=10. So address offset = h*10 + w. Bank = (offset * 4 / 4) % 32 = offset % 32. So as long as (h*10 + w) % 32 across threads in a warp are distinct, no bank conflicts. In a 2D tile of 8x8, there are 64 positions. A warp has 32 threads. If we map threads linearly to (h,w), then the first 32 threads cover positions 0..31 (in some layout). With W=10, the offsets are 0..31 for h=0,w=0..7 (offsets 0..7), h=1,w=0..7 (10..17), h=2,w=0..7 (20..27), h=3,w=0..3 (30..33). So offsets are all distinct modulo 32 for the first 32 threads. Good. The second warp covers h=3,w=4..7 (34..37), h=4,w=0..7 (40..47), etc. Also distinct. So bank conflicts for input loads should be minimal. Good. So storage layout: - input: `float s_in[BLOCK_C][TILE_H+2][TILE_W+2];` - weights: `float s_w[BLOCK_C][9][BLOCK_K];` // transposed Loading weights: all threads load. For each weight index (c, offset, k), compute linear idx and assign to a thread. Since `s_w` size is `BLOCK_C * 9 * BLOCK_K` floats, each thread loads `total / blockDim.x` elements. Each thread writes to a unique location. Since writes are strided across threads, there might be bank conflicts if multiple threads write to the same bank. But since total is relatively small and blockDim.x = 64, each thread writes a few elements. The bank conflict for writes can be reduced by having each thread write consecutive elements in memory. We can map thread linear index `tid` to `tid` consecutive elements. For example: ```cpp int tid = threadIdx.x; // 0..63 int total = BLOCK_C * 9 * BLOCK_K; for (int i = tid; i < total; i += blockDim.x) { int k = i % BLOCK_K; int tmp = i / BLOCK_K; int offset = tmp % 9; int c = tmp / 9; s_w[c][offset][k] = weight[...]; } ``` This is a transposed load from global memory. The global memory read pattern might not be ideal, but weight size is small. Let's check: `BLOCK_C=16`, `BLOCK_K=128`, total = 18432 floats. `blockDim.x=64`. Each thread loads 288 floats. Global memory reads: weights are stored as (K, C, 3, 3) in PyTorch (cuDNN format is NCHW for weights: [K, C, 3, 3]). To load `s_w[c][offset][k]`, we need `weight[k_out, c_in, dy, dx]`. If we iterate over k, then c, then offset, the global access is contiguous for k (since k is the first dimension). In the above mapping `i = ((c * 9 + offset) * BLOCK_K + k)`, we read weight with k varying fastest. For a fixed (c, offset), reading weight[k0..k0+BLOCK_K-1] is contiguous. So global loads are coalesced! Great. Then we write to `s_w[c][offset][k]`, which stores k consecutively. For each thread, consecutive `i` correspond to consecutive `k`. So each thread writes consecutive addresses in shared memory. Different threads write to addresses offset by `blockDim.x` (64) from each other. Since 64 floats = 256 bytes. Shared memory banks are 4 bytes, so 64 floats span 64 banks. With 32 banks, 64 addresses will map to bank = (addr/4) % 32. So consecutive threads writing to addresses 64 apart will have bank = (base/4 + 16) % 32 for thread 1, etc. So thread 0 writes to bank 0,1,2,...; thread 1 writes to bank 16,17,...; thread 2 writes to bank 0,1,... again. So there will be 2-way bank conflicts for writes. That's acceptable. Alternatively, we can reorder or use 128 threads to reduce conflicts. But 2-way is fine. Now, loading input: input is (N, C, H, W). For a given `c_block` and spatial tile, we need to load `BLOCK_C` channels, `TILE_H+2` rows, `TILE_W+2` cols. Global input index: `input[n, c_block + c, h_start + h, w_start + w]`. We can map threads to (c, h, w). Total input tile elements: `BLOCK_C * (TILE_H+2) * (TILE_W+2)`. For BLOCK_C=16, TILE_H=8, TILE_W=8, total = 16*100 = 1600. With 64 threads, each thread loads 25 elements. We can map `tid` linearly over (c, h, w) or over the flat index. If we map `tid` to `idx = tid`, and iterate `i = tid; i < total; i += blockDim.x`, then we compute: ```cpp int c = i / ((TILE_H+2)*(TILE_W+2)); int tmp = i % ((TILE_H+2)*(TILE_W+2)); int h = tmp / (TILE_W+2); int w = tmp % (TILE_W+2); ``` Then read `input[n, c_block + c, h_start + h, w_start + w]`. Global memory coalescing: consecutive `i` correspond to varying `w` fastest. If `TILE_W+2 = 10`, then `w` varies fastest; multiple threads in a warp will load consecutive `w` for same `c` and `h`. With `blockDim.x = 64`, threads 0..63 load `i=0..63`. For `i=0..9`, c=0, h=0, w=0..9. So threads 0..9 load contiguous memory. Threads 10..19 load `i=10..19`, which is c=0, h=1, w=0..9, also contiguous but offset by W in the input. So global loads are coalesced within each row. For padding: if `h_start + h` or `w_start + w` is out of bounds (0..H-1), load 0.0f. Now, the compute phase: Each thread handles a specific output spatial pixel `(out_h, out_w)` within the tile. `out_h` ranges from 0..TILE_H-1, `out_w` 0..TILE_W-1. Now, we need to handle the case where K is not a multiple of BLOCK_K. We can pad or have the kernel handle partial blocks. The easiest is to have the kernel write within bounds checks. We can compile-time specialize the kernel for different tile sizes? Or just use template parameters for TILE_H, TILE_W, BLOCK_K, BLOCK_C, THREADS_PER_PIXEL. But we can write a generic kernel that uses constants defined at compile time via macros or templates. For example: - For layers with H=224: TILE_H=8, TILE_W=8. - For H=112: TILE_H=8, TILE_W=8. - For H=56: TILE_H=8, TILE_W=8 or 4x4. - For H=28: TILE_H=4, TILE_W=4. - For H=14: TILE_H=4, TILE_W=4. So we can have a generic kernel: ```cpp template<int TILE_H, int TILE_W, int BLOCK_K, int BLOCK_C, int THREADS_PER_PIXEL> __global__ void conv3x3_relu_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int K) { ... } ``` In the C++ wrapper, we can call this kernel with parameters chosen based on layer. But since the kernel is a template, we need to instantiate it for the combinations we use. We can just instantiate one version with `TILE_H=8, TILE_W=8, BLOCK_K=64, BLOCK_C=16, THREADS_PER_PIXEL=4` and use it for all layers. This gives blockDim.x = 64*4 = 256. But we need to handle K not multiple of 64. For K=64, okay. K=128, 256, 512: multiple of 64. K=1000 for final layer (but that's linear, not conv). So all conv K values are powers of 2 up to 512. Good. C values: 3, 64, 128, 256, 512. Not all multiples of 16. C=3: we need to handle partial channel blocks. We can add an `if (c < C)` check when loading input and weights. For C=3, when loading weights, `BLOCK_C=16`, but only 3 channels are valid. We can load zeros for the rest. For input, we load zeros for missing channels. Similarly, H and W may not be multiples of TILE_H/TILE_W. We add bounds checks for output writes and input loads. Let's design the kernel carefully. ### Kernel signature: ```cpp template<int TILE_H, int TILE_W, int BLOCK_K, int BLOCK_C, int THREADS_PER_PIXEL> __global__ void conv3x3_relu_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int K) { const int PIXELS_PER_BLOCK = TILE_H * TILE_W; const int THREADS_PER_BLOCK = PIXELS_PER_BLOCK * THREADS_PER_PIXEL; const int K_PER_THREAD = BLOCK_K / THREADS_PER_PIXEL; int tid = threadIdx.x; int pixel_id = tid / THREADS_PER_PIXEL; int k_sub = tid % THREADS_PER_PIXEL; int pixel_h = pixel_id / TILE_W; int pixel_w = pixel_id % TILE_W; int n = blockIdx.z; // batch index int block_k = blockIdx.x * BLOCK_K; int block_h = (blockIdx.y / ((W + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W)) * TILE_H; int block_w = (blockIdx.y % ((W + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W)) * TILE_W; // Or use blockIdx.x for K groups, blockIdx.y for spatial, blockIdx.z for batch. // Let's set grid: dim3(grid_k, grid_spatial, N) // where grid_k = (K + BLOCK_K - 1) / BLOCK_K // grid_spatial = grid_h * grid_w, grid_h = (H + TILE_H - 1)/TILE_H, grid_w = (W + TILE_W - 1)/TILE_W // Compute output position int out_h = block_h + pixel_h; int out_w = block_w + pixel_w; bool valid_out = (out_h < H && out_w < W); // Local accumulation registers float accum[K_PER_THREAD]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K_PER_THREAD; ++i) { int k_idx = block_k + k_sub * K_PER_THREAD + i; accum[i] = (k_idx < K && valid_out) ? bias[k_idx] : 0.0f; } // Shared memory __shared__ float s_in[BLOCK_C][TILE_H + 2][TILE_W + 2]; __shared__ float s_w[BLOCK_C][9][BLOCK_K]; // Compute input tile start (with padding) int in_h_start = block_h - 1; int in_w_start = block_w - 1; // Loop over channel blocks for (int c_block = 0; c_block < C; c_block += BLOCK_C) { // Load input tile into shared memory int total_in = BLOCK_C * (TILE_H + 2) * (TILE_W + 2); for (int i = tid; i < total_in; i += THREADS_PER_BLOCK) { int c = i / ((TILE_H + 2) * (TILE_W + 2)); int tmp = i % ((TILE_H + 2) * (TILE_W + 2)); int h = tmp / (TILE_W + 2); int w = tmp % (TILE_W + 2); int global_c = c_block + c; int global_h = in_h_start + h; int global_w = in_w_start + w; float val = 0.0f; if (global_c < C && global_h >= 0 && global_h < H && global_w >= 0 && global_w < W) { val = input[((n * C + global_c) * H + global_h) * W + global_w]; } s_in[c][h][w] = val; } // Load weights into shared memory int total_w = BLOCK_C * 9 * BLOCK_K; for (int i = tid; i < total_w; i += THREADS_PER_BLOCK) { int k = i % BLOCK_K; int tmp = i / BLOCK_K; int offset = tmp % 9; int c = tmp / 9; int global_c = c_block + c; int global_k = block_k + k; float val = 0.0f; if (global_c < C && global_k < K) { // weight shape: K, C, 3, 3 int dy = offset / 3; int dx = offset % 3; val = weight[((global_k * C + global_c) * 3 + dy) * 3 + dx]; } s_w[c][offset][k] = val; } __syncthreads(); if (valid_out) { // Compute convolution #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < BLOCK_C; ++c) { if (c_block + c >= C) break; #pragma unroll for (int dy = 0; dy < 3; ++dy) { #pragma unroll for (int dx = 0; dx < 3; ++dx) { float inp = s_in[c][pixel_h + dy][pixel_w + dx]; int offset = dy * 3 + dx; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K_PER_THREAD; ++i) { int k = k_sub * K_PER_THREAD + i; accum[i] += inp * s_w[c][offset][k]; } } } } } __syncthreads(); } // Write output if (valid_out) { #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K_PER_THREAD; ++i) { int k_idx = block_k + k_sub * K_PER_THREAD + i; if (k_idx < K) { float val = accum[i]; if (val < 0.0f) val = 0.0f; // ReLU int out_idx = ((n * K + k_idx) * H + out_h) * W + out_w; output[out_idx] = val; } } } } ``` This looks plausible. Let's check the grid dimensions. Grid: - `gridDim.x = (K + BLOCK_K - 1) / BLOCK_K` - `gridDim.y = grid_h * grid_w` - `gridDim.z = N` Where `grid_h = (H + TILE_H - 1) / TILE_H`, `grid_w = (W + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W`. `blockIdx.y` maps to spatial tile: `block_h = (blockIdx.y / grid_w) * TILE_H`, `block_w = (blockIdx.y % grid_w) * TILE_W`. Now, let's evaluate performance for the first conv layer: N=10, C=3, H=224, W=224, K=64. - K blocks: 64/64 = 1. - grid_h = 28, grid_w = 28. Spatial tiles = 784. - N = 10. - Total blocks = 1 * 784 * 10 = 7840. - Threads per block = 256. - Total threads = 7840 * 256 = 2,007,040. This is fine. We can write a simple kernel: ```cpp __global__ void maxpool2x2_kernel( const float* input, float* output, int N, int C, int H, int W) { int n = blockIdx.z; int c = blockIdx.y; int oh = blockIdx.x / ((W+1)/2); int ow = blockIdx.x % ((W+1)/2); int ih = oh * 2; int iw = ow * 2; float max_val = -INFINITY; for (int dy = 0; dy < 2; ++dy) { for (int dx = 0; dx < 2; ++dx) { int h = ih + dy; int w = iw + dx; if (h < H && w < W) { float val = input[((n*C + c)*H + h)*W + w]; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } } } int outH = H / 2; int outW = W / 2; output[((n*C + c)*outH + oh)*outW + ow] = max_val; } ``` Grid: `dim3(ceil(H/2)*ceil(W/2), C, N)`. Actually, we can write a simple linear kernel using a for-loop over N: ```cpp __global__ void linear_relu_forward( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int M, int N, int K, bool relu) { // input: M x N // weight: K x N // output: M x K int k = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (k >= K) return; for (int m = 0; m < M; ++m) { float sum = bias[k]; for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { sum += input[m * N + n] * weight[k * N + n]; } if (relu && sum < 0) sum = 0; output[m * K + k] = sum; } } ``` This is a naive implementation: each thread computes one output column for all M rows. For M=10, each thread does 10 * N FMAs plus loads. For N=25088, that's 250k FMAs per thread. With K=4096, we need 4096 threads. blockDim.x=256 => 16 blocks. Total threads = 16*256 = 4096. When loading input, we check bounds `global_h >= 0 && global_h < H`. So padding is zero. That matches `padding=1` for `nn.Conv2d`. We need to handle bias. PyTorch's Conv2d has bias. We add bias. We need to handle ReLU: `max(0, x)`. Now, let's write the complete C++ and CUDA code. We need to define the custom operations and expose them via `functions=[...]`. The C++ wrapper functions will take `at::Tensor` objects, check contiguous, get pointers, and launch kernels. Let's write the code for `conv3x3_relu_forward`: ```cpp at::Tensor conv3x3_relu_forward( const at::Tensor& input, const at::Tensor& weight, const at::Tensor& bias, int64_t H_out, int64_t W_out) { // input: (N, C, H, W) // weight: (K, C, 3, 3) // bias: (K,) int N = input.size(0); int C = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); int K = weight.size(0); auto output = at::empty({N, K, H, W}, input.options()); const int TILE_H = 16; const int TILE_W = 16; const int BLOCK_K = 64; const int BLOCK_C = 8; const int THREADS_PER_PIXEL = 4; dim3 block(256 * THREADS_PER_PIXEL); // 1024? Wait, 16*16*4=1024 // Actually: PIXELS = 256. THREADS = 256*4 = 1024. dim3 grid((K + BLOCK_K - 1) / BLOCK_K, ((H + TILE_H - 1) / TILE_H) * ((W + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W), N); conv3x3_relu_kernel<TILE_H, TILE_W, BLOCK_K, BLOCK_C, THREADS_PER_PIXEL> <<<grid, block>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, H, W, K); return output; } ``` But we need `conv3x3_relu_kernel` instantiated with these constants. Since they are template parameters, we need to instantiate it or define it in the same compilation unit. Wait, `load_inline` compiles the cuda_sources. We can define the template kernel and call it directly with the constants passed as template arguments. That's fine. No explicit instantiation needed if the call site is in the same translation unit where the template is defined. But `block` dimension: `blockDim.x = 1024`. Is 1024 allowed? Max threads per block is 1024. Yes. `gridDim.y` = grid_h * grid_w. For H=224, grid_h=14, grid_w=14 => 196. So grid.y=196. grid.x up to 8. grid.z=10. Total blocks = 8*196*10 = 15,680 for K=512. That's okay. Now, for smaller H/W (e.g., 14x14), grid_h=1, grid_w=1 => grid.y=1. For K=512, grid.x=8. grid.z=10. Total blocks = 80. That's fine. Now, check `s_in` and `s_w` sizes. `s_in[BLOCK_C][TILE_H+2][TILE_W+2]` = 8*18*18 = 2592. `s_w[BLOCK_C][9][BLOCK_K]` = 8*9*64 = 4608. This fits in 48KB shared memory. Actually, we need to ensure we don't exceed 48KB if we don't request more. But H100 supports up to 227KB. The default is 48KB, but we can request more if needed. Here we only need 28KB, which is fine. Potential issue: `__shared__ float s_in[BLOCK_C][TILE_H+2][TILE_W+2];` requires compile-time constants for dimensions. `BLOCK_C`, `TILE_H`, `TILE_W` are template parameters, so they are compile-time constants. Good. Another issue: `input.data_ptr<float>()` may not be contiguous if the tensor is not channels-last or something. We should ensure contiguous by calling `.contiguous()`. Similarly for weight and bias. In the wrapper, we can do: ```cpp auto input_c = input.contiguous(); auto weight_c = weight.contiguous(); auto bias_c = bias.contiguous(); ``` Now, maxpool kernel: ```cpp at::Tensor maxpool2x2_forward( const at::Tensor& input) { int N = input.size(0); int C = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); int outH = H / 2; int outW = W / 2; auto output = at::empty({N, C, outH, outW}, input.options()); int total = N * C * outH * outW; int threads = 256; int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; maxpool2x2_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, H, W, outH, outW); return output; } ``` Kernel: ```cpp __global__ void maxpool2x2_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int outH, int outW) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * C * outH * outW; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % outW; int h = (idx / outW) % outH; int c = (idx / (outH * outW)) % C; int n = idx / (C * outH * outW); int ih = h * 2; int iw = w * 2; float max_val = -INFINITY; #pragma unroll for (int dy = 0; dy < 2; ++dy) { #pragma unroll for (int dx = 0; dx < 2; ++dx) { int hh = ih + dy; int ww = iw + dx; if (hh < H && ww < W) { float val = input[((n * C + c) * H + hh) * W + ww]; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } } } output[((n * C + c) * outH + h) * outW + w] = max_val; } ``` Now, linear kernels: We can write a generic linear kernel that handles relu optionally. ```cpp at::Tensor linear_forward( const at::Tensor& input, const at::Tensor& weight_t, const at::Tensor& bias, bool relu) { // input: (M, N) // weight_t: (N, K) transposed // bias: (K,) int M = input.size(0); int N = input.size(1); int K = weight_t.size(1); auto output = at::empty({M, K}, input.options()); int threads = 256; int blocks = (K + threads - 1) / threads; linear_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight_t.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), M, N, K, relu); return output; } ``` Kernel with fixed max M: ```cpp __global__ void linear_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight_t, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int M, int N, int K, bool relu) { int k = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (k >= K) return; float sum[16]; #pragma unroll for (int m = 0; m < M; ++m) { sum[m] = bias[k]; } for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { float w = weight_t[n * K + k]; #pragma unroll for (int m = 0; m < M; ++m) { sum[m] += input[m * N + n] * w; } } #pragma unroll for (int m = 0; m < M; ++m) { if (relu && sum[m] < 0.0f) sum[m] = 0.0f; output[m * K + k] = sum[m]; } } ``` But `M` is a runtime variable. The `#pragma unroll` on `for (int m = 0; m < M; ++m)` won't unroll because M is not compile-time. But M is small. We can use a compile-time constant `MAX_M` and pass M: ```cpp template<int MAX_M> __global__ void linear_kernel(...) { int k = ...; if (k >= K) return; float sum[MAX_M]; #pragma unroll for (int m = 0; m < MAX_M; ++m) { if (m < M) sum[m] = bias[k]; } for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { float w = weight_t[n*K + k]; #pragma unroll for (int m = 0; m < MAX_M; ++m) { if (m < M) sum[m] += input[m*N + n] * w; } } #pragma unroll for (int m = 0; m < MAX_M; ++m) { if (m < M) { if (relu && sum[m] < 0.0f) sum[m] = 0.0f; output[m*K + k] = sum[m]; } } } ``` But we can just declare `float sum[16];` and loop `for (int m=0; m<M; ++m)`; the compiler will likely keep it in registers since M<=16 and it's a small array. Or we can just do `#define MAX_M 32` and use a fixed-size array. Alternatively, to handle arbitrary M (though here it's 10), we can call a template instantiation for M=10. But M is dynamic. We can write a switch: ```cpp switch (M) { case 1: linear_kernel_fixed<1><<<...>>>(...); break; case 2: linear_kernel_fixed<2><<<...>>>(...); break; // ... case 16: linear_kernel_fixed<16><<<...>>>(...); break; default: linear_kernel_generic<<<...>>>(...); } ``` But M=10. We can just handle up to 16 with a switch. That's fine. However, there is a risk that `float sum[16]` with dynamic indexing based on `n` inside a loop might cause register spilling if the compiler can't prove the indices are static. But since the loops over m are small and independent, the compiler should keep them in registers. To be safe, we can manually unroll for MAX_M=16. But a generic template is fine. I think for simplicity we can define `MAX_M=32` and just loop. H100 has many registers. Now, we need to transpose FC weights. In `ModelNew.__init__`, after creating the linear layers, we can create transposed buffers: ```python self.fc1_weight_t = nn.Parameter(self.classifier[0].weight.t().contiguous()) self.fc1_bias = self.classifier[0].bias ... ``` But if we create new Parameters, the state dict won't match. We can instead just store them as attributes and not register them as parameters. But when copying state dict, the original `classifier.0.weight` will be copied, and we can compute the transposed version in `forward` on the fly or in `__init__`. Since `__init__` is called after the module is created, we can compute `self.classifier[0].weight.t().contiguous()` inside `forward`? That's expensive. Better to compute it once in `__init__` and store as a buffer. ```python self.register_buffer('fc1_w', self.classifier[0].weight.t().contiguous(), persistent=False) ``` But state dict will include `fc1_w` if persistent. `persistent=False` excludes it from state dict. Alternatively, we can avoid transposing by storing the weight as (K, N) and reading it transposed in the kernel: `weight[n * K + k]`. This is already what we'd have if we store weight as (N, K). But PyTorch stores linear weight as (K, N). So we need to read `weight[k * N + n]`. In the naive kernel, each thread reads n sequentially for fixed k. That's `weight[k*N + n]` which is contiguous in n. Wait, yes! If weight is (K, N) row-major, then for fixed k, `weight[k*N + n]` is contiguous! So we don't need to transpose! In the naive kernel: ```cpp for (int n = 0; n < N; ++n) { float w = weight[k * N + n]; ... } ``` This is a contiguous read (sequential) within each thread! There's no coalescing across threads because each thread reads a different k. But sequential reads within a thread are efficient and use cache well. Since each thread reads N floats (e.g., 25088) contiguously, the memory system will prefetch nicely. L2 cache will hold the row. Multiple threads reading different rows might cause some L2 thrashing, but GPU memory has enough bandwidth. Actually, is sequential per thread good enough? Global memory loads from a warp are coalesced if threads access contiguous addresses. Here, thread `tid` reads `weight[(k_block+tid)*N + n]`. For a fixed n, threads read addresses separated by N floats (e.g., 100KB). That's not contiguous. So memory loads are not coalesced; they are strided. This is bad for memory bandwidth. However, if N is large, each load is to a different cache line. With 32 threads in a warp, we'd load 32 cache lines (4KB each? No, 128 bytes). Actually each cache line is 128 bytes = 32 floats. So each thread loads a cache line every 32 floats. The addresses are spaced by N floats. The warp issues 32 memory requests. The memory controller can handle many concurrent requests. But the effective bandwidth for strided accesses is lower than contiguous. To improve, we can have the kernel load weight in a coalesced manner by reading `weight[k * N + n]` with k varying across threads and n constant. Instead of having each thread loop over n, we can loop over n and have the warp load a strip of weight for all k. For example, we can have each block load `weight[k_start..k_end][n]` into shared memory for a given n. Then threads read from shared memory. This is exactly a standard GEMM approach. But for simplicity and given M=10, maybe strided access is okay because total traffic is small. Let's compute effective bandwidth. For FC1, weight traffic = 400MB. At 50% effective bandwidth (due to striding), that's 800MB. Time = 0.24ms. Still fine. But to be more efficient, we can read transposed weight (N, K) which gives coalesced loads across threads. Let's see: `weight_t[n*K + k]`. For fixed n, threads read contiguous addresses. That's perfectly coalesced. So we should transpose. To avoid state dict issues, we can store the transposed weight as a non-persistent buffer. When the state dict is loaded, the original weight gets updated. In `forward`, we can check if the transposed buffer is stale and recompute if needed. How to check? Another option: use `torch.addmm` inside `forward` for FC layers, but use `torch` operations from the compiled extension? Actually, we can write the linear kernel to read weight in row-major (K,N) and use shared memory to transpose on the fly. We could load a tile of weight into shared memory in a coalesced way. Specifically, we can tile over N and K. For a block computing K_tile output channels, we process N in tiles of BLOCK_N. For each n_tile, we load `weight[k_start + tid_k][n_start + tid_n]` into shared memory. Since `weight` is (K, N), the load is coalesced if warp threads read contiguous n for a given k. Let's design the full `ModelNew.forward`: ```python def forward(self, x): # x: (N, 3, 224, 224) # Block 1 x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[0].weight, self.features[0].bias, 224, 224) x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[2].weight, self.features[2].bias, 224, 224) x = maxpool2x2_forward(x) # Actually, `torch.addmm(input, mat1, mat2, *, beta=1, alpha=1, out=None)` computes `beta * input + alpha * mat1 @ mat2`. The first argument is the bias-like term. But if we pass `bias` as the first argument, we need to ensure shapes broadcast. `bias` is (out_features,). `input` is (M, in). `weight.t()` is (in, out). `mat1 @ mat2` is (M, out). `bias` broadcasts to (M, out). The weight tensor shape from `nn.Conv2d` is (K, C, 3, 3). In memory, it's row-major: weight[k][c][dy][dx]. So `((global_k * C + global_c) * 3 + dy) * 3 + dx` is correct. Bias shape: (K,). `bias[k]`. Input shape: (N, C, H, W). Index: `((n * C + c) * H + h) * W + w`. Output shape: (N, K, H, W). Index: `((n * K + k) * H + h) * W + w`. Now, maxpool: Input (N, C, H, W). Output (N, C, H/2, W/2). Now, we need to ensure that we include the necessary headers and use `AT_CHECK` or `TORCH_CHECK`. Let's draft the complete code. ### Draft of Python file with kernel ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <float.h> template<int TILE_H, int TILE_W, int BLOCK_K, int BLOCK_C, int THREADS_PER_PIXEL> __global__ void conv3x3_relu_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C, int H, int W, int K) { const int PIXELS_PER_BLOCK = TILE_H * TILE_W; const int THREADS_PER_BLOCK = PIXELS_PER_BLOCK * THREADS_PER_PIXEL; const int K_PER_THREAD = BLOCK_K / THREADS_PER_PIXEL; int tid = threadIdx.x; int pixel_id = tid / THREADS_PER_PIXEL; int k_sub = tid % THREADS_PER_PIXEL; int pixel_h = pixel_id / TILE_W; int pixel_w = pixel_id % TILE_W; int n = blockIdx.z; int block_k = blockIdx.x * BLOCK_K; int grid_w = (W + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W; int block_h = (blockIdx.y / grid_w) * TILE_H; int block_w = (blockIdx.y % grid_w) * TILE_W; int out_h = block_h + pixel_h; int out_w = block_w + pixel_w; bool valid_out = (out_h < H && out_w < W); float accum[K_PER_THREAD]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K_PER_THREAD; ++i) { int k_idx = block_k + k_sub * K_PER_THREAD + i; accum[i] = (valid_out && k_idx < K) ? bias[k_idx] : 0.0f; } __shared__ float s_in[BLOCK_C][TILE_H + 2][TILE_W + 2]; __shared__ float s_w[BLOCK_C][9][BLOCK_K]; int in_h_start = block_h - 1; int in_w_start = block_w - 1; for (int c_block = 0; c_block < C; c_block += BLOCK_C) { // Load input tile int total_in = BLOCK_C * (TILE_H + 2) * (TILE_W + 2); for (int i = tid; i < total_in; i += THREADS_PER_BLOCK) { int c = i / ((TILE_H + 2) * (TILE_W + 2)); int tmp = i % ((TILE_H + 2) * (TILE_W + 2)); int h = tmp / (TILE_W + 2); int w = tmp % (TILE_W + 2); int global_c = c_block + c; int global_h = in_h_start + h; int global_w = in_w_start + w; float val = 0.0f; if (global_c < C && global_h >= 0 && global_h < H && global_w >= 0 && global_w < W) { val = input[((n * C + global_c) * H + global_h) * W + global_w]; } } } } } __syncthreads(); } if (valid_out) { #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K_PER_THREAD; ++i) { int k_idx = block_k + k_sub * K_PER_THREAD + i; if (k_idx < K) { float val = accum[i]; if (val < 0.0f) val = 0.0f; One concern: The `conv3x3_relu_kernel` uses `__shared__ float s_in[BLOCK_C][TILE_H+2][TILE_W+2];` and `__shared__ float s_w[BLOCK_C][9][BLOCK_K];`. However, C++ templates require the array dimensions to be compile-time constants, which they are. NVCC should compile this fine. Another concern: `float accum[K_PER_THREAD];` where `K_PER_THREAD = BLOCK_K / THREADS_PER_PIXEL = 64/4 = 16`. This is a compile-time constant (since all template args are ints). So it's a VLA? Actually, `K_PER_THREAD` is a `const int` initialized by a constant expression. In C++, local arrays with size determined by a `const int` are allowed (VLA is C99, not standard C++). But since it's compiled by NVCC which supports some extensions, it might be okay. To be safe, we can use a fixed maximum size and loop accordingly. But since K_PER_THREAD is compile-time constant (constexpr), we can declare it as `static constexpr int K_PER_THREAD = BLOCK_K / THREADS_PER_PIXEL;` inside the kernel. Actually, inside a function, `static constexpr` is allowed in C++11. But CUDA kernels are device functions. Let's just use `#define` or a `constexpr` inside the kernel body: ```cpp constexpr int K_PER_THREAD = BLOCK_K / THREADS_PER_PIXEL; float accum[K_PER_THREAD]; ``` Since `BLOCK_K` and `THREADS_PER_PIXEL` are template parameters, `BLOCK_K / THREADS_PER_PIXEL` is a constant expression. NVCC might accept this as array size. I think NVCC does support this. To be safe, we can allocate an array of size 64 (max) and only use K_PER_THREAD. Or we can not use an array and unroll manually with `#pragma unroll`. But using an array is cleaner. The index calculation is `s_w[c][offset][k]`. In C/C++, multi-dimensional arrays are stored row-major with the last dimension contiguous. So `s_w[c][offset][k]` accesses contiguous memory for varying `k`. Good. Similarly, `s_in[c][h][w]` is contiguous in `w`. Good. Now, we need to make sure that the weight loading loop writes to `s_w` correctly. We map linear index `i` to `(c, offset, k)` such that consecutive `i` correspond to consecutive `k`. Then consecutive threads write to consecutive `k` in `s_w`. But the mapping is `c = tmp / 9`, `offset = tmp % 9`, `k = i % BLOCK_K`. So `k` varies fastest. For a given `c` and `offset`, `i` from `base` to `base+BLOCK_K-1` are written by consecutive `i` values. Threads with `tid` and `tid+1` might write `i` and `i+1` if `THREADS_PER_BLOCK` divides `BLOCK_K`? No, `i` increments by `THREADS_PER_BLOCK` (1024). So thread 0 writes i=0,1024,...; thread 1 writes i=1,1025,... So threads write to locations scattered across `s_w`. The writes are to different elements. Bank conflicts? As analyzed earlier, 2-way conflict is okay. Could we improve by having each thread write a contiguous chunk? For example, `int base = tid * (total_w / THREADS_PER_BLOCK);` But `total_w` might not be divisible. Simpler to keep the strided loop. reasoning
The issue is that the CUDA source contains the function definitions, but the C++ source is used for generating the pybind11 wrappers. Since the cpp_sources is empty, the function declarations need to be present somewhere that the generated main.cpp can find them. The problem is that the CUDA source file gets compiled separately, and the C++ compiler doesn't see those declarations. I need to provide a `cpp_sources` header with forward declarations of the CUDA functions that will be bound. Also, the functions in `functions=[...]` need to match the function names in the pybind11 module. Let me fix this.

In PyTorch's `cpp_extension.py`, the function `_jit_compile` is called. For inline extensions: ```python def _write_inline_source_and_build_meta(name, cpp_sources, cuda_sources, functions, ...): build_directory = ... cpp_path = os.path.join(build_directory, f'{name}.cpp') with open(cpp_path, 'w') as f: f.write(cpp_sources) if cuda_sources: cuda_path = os.path.join(build_directory, 'cuda.cu') with open(cuda_path, 'w') as f: f.write(cuda_sources) sources = [cpp_path] if cuda_sources: sources.append(cuda_path) # Generate bindings inline.h # ... ``` Then `_jit_compile` generates main.cpp from the `functions` list and includes `inline.h` (or similar). In the binding generation: ```python def _jit_compile(name, sources, ...): ... with_cuda = any(map(_is_cuda_file, sources)) ... # Create bindings file if needed if is_python_module: bindings_file = os.path.join(build_directory, f'{name}_bindings.cpp') with open(bindings_file, 'w') as f: f.write(_generate_bindings(functions, with_cuda)) sources.append(bindings_file) ... ``` And `_generate_bindings` does: ```python def _generate_bindings(functions, with_cuda): lines = [] lines.append('#include <torch/extension.h>') for func in functions: lines.append(f'torch::Tensor {func}(...);') # Auto-declare? ... ``` No, it doesn't auto-declare. import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cpp_source = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
torch::Tensor conv3x3_relu_forward_cuda(
    const torch::Tensor& input,
    const torch::Tensor& weight,
    const torch::Tensor& bias);

torch::Tensor maxpool2x2_forward_cuda(const torch::Tensor& input);
'''

cuda_source = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <cuda_runtime.h>
#include <float.h>

template<int TILE_H, int TILE_W, int BLOCK_K, int BLOCK_C, int THREADS_PER_PIXEL>
__global__ void conv3x3_relu_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ input,
    const float* __restrict__ weight,
    const float* __restrict__ bias,
    float* __restrict__ output,
    int N, int C, int H, int W, int K) {
    const int PIXELS_PER_BLOCK = TILE_H * TILE_W;
    const int THREADS_PER_BLOCK = PIXELS_PER_BLOCK * THREADS_PER_PIXEL;
    constexpr int K_PER_THREAD = BLOCK_K / THREADS_PER_PIXEL;

    int tid = threadIdx.x;
    int pixel_id = tid / THREADS_PER_PIXEL;
    int k_sub = tid % THREADS_PER_PIXEL;

    int pixel_h = pixel_id / TILE_W;
    int pixel_w = pixel_id % TILE_W;

    int n = blockIdx.z;
    int block_k = blockIdx.x * BLOCK_K;
    int grid_w = (W + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W;
    int block_h = (blockIdx.y / grid_w) * TILE_H;
    int block_w = (blockIdx.y % grid_w) * TILE_W;

    int out_h = block_h + pixel_h;
    int out_w = block_w + pixel_w;
    bool valid_out = (out_h < H && out_w < W);

    float accum[K_PER_THREAD];
    #pragma unroll
    for (int i = 0; i < K_PER_THREAD; ++i) {
        int k_idx = block_k + k_sub * K_PER_THREAD + i;
        accum[i] = (valid_out && k_idx < K) ? bias[k_idx] : 0.0f;
    }

    __shared__ float s_in[BLOCK_C][TILE_H + 2][TILE_W + 2];
    __shared__ float s_w[BLOCK_C][9][BLOCK_K];

    int in_h_start = block_h - 1;
    int in_w_start = block_w - 1;

    for (int c_block = 0; c_block < C; c_block += BLOCK_C) {
        int total_in = BLOCK_C * (TILE_H + 2) * (TILE_W + 2);
        for (int i = tid; i < total_in; i += THREADS_PER_BLOCK) {
            int c = i / ((TILE_H + 2) * (TILE_W + 2));
            int tmp = i % ((TILE_H + 2) * (TILE_W + 2));
            int h = tmp / (TILE_W + 2);
            int w = tmp % (TILE_W + 2);

            int global_c = c_block + c;
            int global_h = in_h_start + h;
            int global_w = in_w_start + w;

            float val = 0.0f;
            if (global_c < C && global_h >= 0 && global_h < H && global_w >= 0 && global_w < W) {
                val = input[((n * C + global_c) * H + global_h) * W + global_w];
            }
            s_in[c][h][w] = val;
        }

        int total_w = BLOCK_C * 9 * BLOCK_K;
        for (int i = tid; i < total_w; i += THREADS_PER_BLOCK) {
            int k = i % BLOCK_K;
            int tmp = i / BLOCK_K;
            int offset = tmp % 9;
            int c = tmp / 9;

            int global_c = c_block + c;
            int global_k = block_k + k;

            float val = 0.0f;
            if (global_c < C && global_k < K) {
                int dy = offset / 3;
                int dx = offset % 3;
                val = weight[((global_k * C + global_c) * 3 + dy) * 3 + dx];
            }
            s_w[c][offset][k] = val;
        }

        __syncthreads();

        if (valid_out) {
            #pragma unroll
            for (int c = 0; c < BLOCK_C; ++c) {
                if (c_block + c >= C) break;
                #pragma unroll
                for (int dy = 0; dy < 3; ++dy) {
                    #pragma unroll
                    for (int dx = 0; dx < 3; ++dx) {
                        float inp = s_in[c][pixel_h + dy][pixel_w + dx];
                        int offset = dy * 3 + dx;
                        #pragma unroll
                        for (int i = 0; i < K_PER_THREAD; ++i) {
                            int k = k_sub * K_PER_THREAD + i;
                            accum[i] += inp * s_w[c][offset][k];
                        }
                    }
                }
            }
        }

        __syncthreads();
    }

    if (valid_out) {
        #pragma unroll
        for (int i = 0; i < K_PER_THREAD; ++i) {
            int k_idx = block_k + k_sub * K_PER_THREAD + i;
            if (k_idx < K) {
                float val = accum[i];
                if (val < 0.0f) val = 0.0f;
                output[((n * K + k_idx) * H + out_h) * W + out_w] = val;
            }
        }
    }
}

torch::Tensor conv3x3_relu_forward_cuda(
    const torch::Tensor& input,
    const torch::Tensor& weight,
    const torch::Tensor& bias) {
    auto input_c = input.contiguous();
    auto weight_c = weight.contiguous();
    auto bias_c = bias.contiguous();

    int N = input_c.size(0);
    int C = input_c.size(1);
    int H = input_c.size(2);
    int W = input_c.size(3);
    int K = weight_c.size(0);

    auto output = torch::empty({N, K, H, W}, input_c.options());

    const int TILE_H = 14;
    const int TILE_W = 14;
    const int BLOCK_K = 64;
    const int BLOCK_C = 4;
    const int THREADS_PER_PIXEL = 4;

    dim3 block(THREADS_PER_PIXEL * TILE_H * TILE_W);
    dim3 grid(
        (K + BLOCK_K - 1) / BLOCK_K,
        ((H + TILE_H - 1) / TILE_H) * ((W + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W),
        N
    );

    conv3x3_relu_kernel<TILE_H, TILE_W, BLOCK_K, BLOCK_C, THREADS_PER_PIXEL>
        <<<grid, block>>>(
            input_c.data_ptr<float>(),
            weight_c.data_ptr<float>(),
            bias_c.data_ptr<float>(),
            output.data_ptr<float>(),
            N, C, H, W, K);

    return output;
}

__global__ void maxpool2x2_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ input,
    float* __restrict__ output,
    int N, int C, int H, int W, int outH, int outW) {
    int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;
    int total = N * C * outH * outW;
    if (idx >= total) return;

    int w = idx % outW;
    int h = (idx / outW) % outH;
    int c = (idx / (outH * outW)) % C;
    int n = idx / (C * outH * outW);

    int ih = h * 2;
    int iw = w * 2;
    float max_val = -FLT_MAX;
    #pragma unroll
    for (int dy = 0; dy < 2; ++dy) {
        #pragma unroll
        for (int dx = 0; dx < 2; ++dx) {
            int hh = ih + dy;
            int ww = iw + dx;
            if (hh < H && ww < W) {
                float val = input[((n * C + c) * H + hh) * W + ww];
                if (val > max_val) max_val = val;
            }
        }
    }
    output[((n * C + c) * outH + h) * outW + w] = max_val;
}

torch::Tensor maxpool2x2_forward_cuda(const torch::Tensor& input) {
    auto input_c = input.contiguous();
    int N = input_c.size(0);
    int C = input_c.size(1);
    int H = input_c.size(2);
    int W = input_c.size(3);
    int outH = H / 2;
    int outW = W / 2;
    auto output = torch::empty({N, C, outH, outW}, input_c.options());

    int total = N * C * outH * outW;
    int threads = 256;
    int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads;

    maxpool2x2_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(
        input_c.data_ptr<float>(),
        output.data_ptr<float>(),
        N, C, H, W, outH, outW);

    return output;
}
'''

module = load_inline(
    name='vggcuda9',
    cpp_sources=cpp_source,
    cuda_sources=cuda_source,
    functions=['conv3x3_relu_forward_cuda', 'maxpool2x2_forward_cuda'],
    extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'],
    extra_ldflags=['-static-libstdc++'],
    verbose=False,
)

conv3x3_relu_forward = module.conv3x3_relu_forward_cuda
maxpool2x2_forward = module.maxpool2x2_forward_cuda

class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, num_classes=1000):
        super(ModelNew, self).__init__()
        self.features = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Conv2d(3, 64, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(64, 64, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2),
            nn.Conv2d(64, 128, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(128, 128, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2),
            nn.Conv2d(128, 256, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(256, 256, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(256, 256, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(256, 256, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2),
            nn.Conv2d(256, 512, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(512, 512, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(512, 512, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(512, 512, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2),
            nn.Conv2d(512, 512, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(512, 512, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(512, 512, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(512, 512, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2)
        )
        self.classifier = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Linear(512 * 7 * 7, 4096),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Dropout(p=0.0),
            nn.Linear(4096, 4096),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Dropout(p=0.0),
            nn.Linear(4096, num_classes)
        )

    def forward(self, x):
        x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[0].weight, self.features[0].bias)
        x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[2].weight, self.features[2].bias)
        x = maxpool2x2_forward(x)
        x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[5].weight, self.features[5].bias)
        x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[7].weight, self.features[7].bias)
        x = maxpool2x2_forward(x)
        x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[10].weight, self.features[10].bias)
        x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[12].weight, self.features[12].bias)
        x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[14].weight, self.features[14].bias)
        x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[16].weight, self.features[16].bias)
        x = maxpool2x2_forward(x)
        x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[19].weight, self.features[19].bias)
        x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[21].weight, self.features[21].bias)
        x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[23].weight, self.features[23].bias)
        x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[25].weight, self.features[25].bias)
        x = maxpool2x2_forward(x)
        x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[28].weight, self.features[28].bias)
        x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[30].weight, self.features[30].bias)
        x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[32].weight, self.features[32].bias)
        x = conv3x3_relu_forward(x, self.features[34].weight, self.features[34].bias)
        x = maxpool2x2_forward(x)
        x = torch.flatten(x, 1)
        x = torch.addmm(self.classifier[0].bias, x, self.classifier[0].weight.t())
        x = torch.relu(x)
        x = torch.addmm(self.classifier[3].bias, x, self.classifier[3].weight.t())
        x = torch.relu(x)
        x = torch.addmm(self.classifier[6].bias, x, self.classifier[6].weight.t())
        return x
